<span class="italic"><a title="Link to journal home page" href="https://doi.org/10.1039/2046-2069/2011">RSC Adv.</a></span>, 2022, <strong>12</strong>, 12383-12395</div><h1 id="sect205"><span class="title_heading">An insight into the sodium-ion and lithium-ion storage properties of CuS/graphitic carbon nitride nanocomposite<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a></span></h1><p class="header_text">
<span class="bold">Dimple P. Dutta</span><span class="orcid"><a target="_blank" title="Select to open ORCID record for Dimple P.&nbsp;Dutta (orcid.org/0000-0001-8955-9166) in a new window" id="connect-orcid-link" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-8955-9166"><img id="orcid-id-logo" src="/content/NewImages/orcid_16x16.png" alt="ORCID logo"></a></span>*<sup><span class="sup_ref italic">ab</span></sup>, 
<span class="bold">Dipa D. Pathak</span><span class="orcid"><a target="_blank" title="Select to open ORCID record for Dipa D.&nbsp;Pathak (orcid.org/0000-0003-0875-6841) in a new window" id="connect-orcid-link" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0003-0875-6841"><img id="orcid-id-logo" src="/content/NewImages/orcid_16x16.png" alt="ORCID logo"></a></span><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">a</span></sup>, 
<span class="bold">Sebin Abraham</span><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">c</span></sup><span class="bold"> and </span>
<span class="bold">Balaji R. Ravuri</span><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">d</span></sup>
<br><a id="affa"><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">a</span></sup></a><span class="italic">Chemistry Division, Bhabha Atomic Research Centre, Mumbai 400 085, India. E-mail: <a href="mailto:dimpled@barc.gov.in">dimpled@barc.gov.in</a>;   Tel: +91-25592308</span>
<br><a id="affb"><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">b</span></sup></a><span class="italic">Homi Bhabha National Institute, Mumbai 400094, India</span>
<br><a id="affc"><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">c</span></sup></a><span class="italic">Department of Chemistry, Indian Institute of Science Education and Research, Bhopal 462066, India</span>
<br><a id="affd"><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">d</span></sup></a><span class="italic">Department of Physics, School of Science, GITAM Deemed to be University, Hyderabad 502329, India</span>
</p><div id="art-admin"><span class="italic bold">Received 
29th March 2022
</span><span class="bold italic">, Accepted 13th April 2022</span></div><p class="bold italic">First published on 25th April 2022</p><hr><div class="abstract"><h2>Abstract</h2><p>Metal sulfides are gaining prominence as conversion anode materials for lithium/sodium ion batteries due to their higher specific capacities but suffers from low stability and reversibility issues. In this work, the electrochemical properties of CuS anode material has been successfully enhanced by its composite formation using graphitic carbon nitride (g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small>). The CuS nanoparticles are distributed evenly in the exfoliated g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> matrix rendering higher electronic conductivity and space for volume alterations during the repeated discharge/charge cycles. The 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite when used as an anode for lithium ion coin cell exhibits a reversible capacity of 478.4 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at a current rate of 2.0 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small> after a run of 1000 cycles which is better than that reported for CuS composites with any other carbon-based matrix. The performance is equally impressive when 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite is used as an anode in a sodium ion coin cell and a reversible capacity of 408 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> is obtained at a current rate of 2.0 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small> after a run of 800 cycles. A sodium ion full cell with NVP cathode and 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite anode has been fabricated and cycled for 100 runs at a current rate of 0.1 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>. It can be inferred that the g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> matrix improves the ion transfer properties, alleviates the volume alteration happening in the anode during the discharge/charge process and also helps in preventing the leaching of polysulfides generated during the electrochemical process.</p></div><hr>
<h2 id="sect287"><span class="a_heading">Introduction</span></h2>
<span>Research on electrochemical energy storage particularly rechargeable batteries has gained tremendous momentum in the last two decades primarily for its seminal role in the field of portable electronics.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit1"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1</span></sup></a> Among various rechargeable battery technologies, the advancement in lithium ion batteries (LIBs) has been the most dramatic.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit2"><sup><span class="sup_ref">2</span></sup></a> Further impetus in LIB technology is being brought about by its application in electric vehicles and renewable energy storage.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit3"><sup><span class="sup_ref">3</span></sup></a> However, the rise in demand for lithium which is scantily available (20 ppm) and geopolitically inequitably distributed in the earth's crust is escalating its price and leading to an impending crisis.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit4"><sup><span class="sup_ref">4</span></sup></a> Consequently, there has been a surge of exploration in alternative technologies like those using the more abundant element sodium which can lead to less expensive sodium ion batteries (SIBs).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit5"><sup><span class="sup_ref">5</span></sup></a> However, the cost advantage of SIBs over LIBs is somewhat thwarted by the sluggish kinetics of the comparatively larger and heavier Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> ions and its higher standard electrode potential (2.71 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> SHE) compared to Li<small><sup>+</sup></small> ions.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit6"><sup><span class="sup_ref">6</span></sup></a> Nonetheless, it has been predicted that SIBs are poised to play a major role in the renewable energy storage sector where some laxity in gravimetric energy density is tolerable.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit7"><sup><span class="sup_ref">7</span></sup></a> The capacity of both LIBs and SIBs is majorly dependent on the properties of the materials used as electrode (cathode and anode). For currently available commercial LIBs, the standard anode material is graphite which has a theoretical capacity of 372 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit3"><sup><span class="sup_ref">3</span></sup></a> However, for its application in electric vehicles, LIBs need to improve its energy density for which alternative anode materials needs to be identified. In case of SIB, the anode material of choice is hard carbon since it has higher Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> transport capacity and its chemical potential almost matches with that of Na metal.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit8"><sup><span class="sup_ref">8</span></sup></a> However, the drawback of hard carbon anode is its low initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) and poor rate performance.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit9"><sup><span class="sup_ref">9</span></sup></a> Hence, there is a need to explore better anode materials with higher specific capacity, extended cyclability and adequate rate performance, for both LIBs and SIBs.</span>
<p class="otherpara">The conversion type anodes generally have higher capacity compared to insertion type materials like graphite and hard carbon. Transition metal oxides, sulfides, phosphides and nitrides have been explored as conversion type anodes for both LIBs and SIBs.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit10"><sup><span class="sup_ref">10</span></sup></a> The conversion reaction leads to the formation of the corresponding Li/Na salt (oxide, sulphide, phosphide, nitride) with concomitant release of the transition metal during the lithiation/sodiation process. The transition metal sulfides have lower ionicity which helps in smoother insertion/de-insertion of the Li<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> ions and reduces the overpotential linked to ion storage.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit11"><sup><span class="sup_ref">11</span></sup></a> The smaller band gap in transition metal sulfides compared to their oxide counterparts, enhances the electronic conductivity of the former which is also advantageous in the context of electrochemical energy storage.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit12"><sup><span class="sup_ref">12</span></sup></a> Among the various transition metal sulfides, inexpensive and abundantly available CuS gains advantage as an anode material due to its moderately high theoretical capacity (560 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>) and electronic conductivity (10<small><sup>3</sup></small> S cm<small><sup>1</sup></small>).<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit13"><sup><span class="sup_ref">13,14</span></sup></a> However, its performance is marred by the volume expansion/contraction which occurs during the cycling process and leads to pulverization of the anode material and its consequent stripping off from the current collector strip.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit15"><sup><span class="sup_ref">15</span></sup></a> Also, an added point of concern is that the polysulfides formed during the conversion reaction dissolves in the electrolyte leading to loss of the active material.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit16"><sup><span class="sup_ref">16</span></sup></a> Both these factors result in poor cycling performance of CuS and hence various strategies has been adopted over the years to circumvent these problems. Different types of nanoforms of CuS have been studied to compensate for the pulverization effect and suitable coating with conducting materials have been attempted to alleviate the deleterious effect of volume expansion and also improve the electronic conductivity of the resultant anode.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit15"><sup><span class="sup_ref">15</span></sup></a> There are reports on the electrochemical properties of CuS nano-particles/sheets/plates, as well as porous and hollow CuS, when used as anodes for LIBs and SIBs.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit13"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1315,17</span></sup></a> To recompense for the volume expansion effect and further improve the electronic conductivity of CuS, composites with graphene, graphene-oxide, reduced graphene-oxide, MOF-derived carbon, carbon nanotubes, have also been explored as anode materials.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit14"><sup><span class="sup_ref">14,1823</span></sup></a> However, synthesis of all of these conductive additives is complex and not cost effective.</p>
<p class="otherpara">g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> has a layered structure similar to graphene and can be synthesized from inexpensive precursors like melamine, urea, <span class="italic">etc.</span> by simple thermal treatment.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit24"><sup><span class="sup_ref">24</span></sup></a> The higher concentration of nitrogen (57%) in g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> results in its lower electronic conductivity compared to that of carbon which hinders its application for Li<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> ion storage.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit25"><sup><span class="sup_ref">25</span></sup></a> However, modified g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> anodes with greater proportion of terminal pyridinic nitrogen bonds compared to quaternary nitrogen and also its composite with carbon and reduced graphene oxide has been explored as anode material for LIB and SIB and has shown promising results.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit24"><sup><span class="sup_ref">24,26,27</span></sup></a> Improvement in specific capacity of mesoporous CuO has been reported on its composite formation with O-doped g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> nanospheres with a concomitant increase in capacity retention from 47% to 75.3% in the latter.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit28"><sup><span class="sup_ref">28</span></sup></a> However, corresponding studies on the electrochemical properties of transition metal sulfide composites with g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> is relatively unexplored.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit12"><sup><span class="sup_ref">12,29</span></sup></a></p>
<p class="otherpara">CuS composites with g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> has been studied mainly for their application as visible light active photocatalysts for degradation of organic pollutants and for water splitting.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit30"><sup><span class="sup_ref">3032</span></sup></a> In this manuscript, the electrochemical properties of CuS/g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite synthesized using simple and scalable precipitation technique has been investigated for Li<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> ion storage and has been compared with that reported for their pristine counterpart. The reason behind the enhanced electrochemical properties of CuS/g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite has been analysed critically and the pivotal role played by the g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> matrix has been explained through adequate experimental techniques.</p>

<h2 id="sect351"><span class="a_heading">Experimental</span></h2>
<h3 id="sect353"><span class="b_heading">Material synthesis</span></h3>
<span>All the chemicals used for synthesis of the anode material and fabrication of electrodes were of Analytical Reagent grade and was used without any additional purification process.</span>

<h3 id="sect356"><span class="b_heading">Synthesis of CuS</span></h3>
<span>In a typical synthesis procedure, aqueous solution (35 ml) of copper(<span class="small_caps">II</span>) nitrate trihydrate (2 g, 8.28 mmol) was taken in a RB flask under N<small><sub>2</sub></small> and aqueous solution of sodium thiosulphate (1.31 g, 8.28 mmol) was added to it dropwise. Deionized (DI) water was used to make the solutions. The blend was stirred vigorously at 90 C for 7 h. The black precipitate attained was separated by centrifugation, then washed repeatedly with deionised water and dried under vacuum.</span>

<h3 id="sect361"><span class="b_heading">Synthesis of g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small></span></h3>
<span>Urea (6 g) was used as a precursor for the synthesis of g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small>. It was heated in an alumina crucible in a muffle furnace at 500 C for 2 h. The product obtained was faint yellow in color. It was sonicated with 20 ml nitric acid for exfoliation and then washed repeatedly with water/ethanol mixture and finally dehydrated in an oven at 70 C for 6 h.</span>

<h3 id="sect368"><span class="b_heading">Synthesis of 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite</span></h3>
<span>g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> (0.19 g, 2.06 mmol) was dispersed in 30 ml deionized water. The dispersed mixture was poured in a N<small><sub>2</sub></small> flushed RB flask and aqueous solution of copper(<span class="small_caps">II</span>) nitrate trihydrate (2 g, 8.28 mmol) was added to it. An aqueous solution of sodium thiosulphate (1.26 g, 8.28 mmol) was added dropwise to the mixture and stirred at 90 C for 7 h. The precipitate obtained was separated out by centrifugation, cleaned with DI water thrice and then vacuum dried.</span>

<h3 id="sect377"><span class="b_heading">Physical characterization</span></h3>
<span>The powder X-ray diffraction (XRD) diffraction patterns were obtained on a Rigaku Smart Lab XRD device (10  2<span class="italic"></span>  80, scan rate: 0.02 s<small><sup>1</sup></small>) using Cu-K<small><sub></sub></small> radiation (<span class="italic"></span> = 1.5406 ). SEM (scanning electron microscopy) images were recorded on Philips XL 300 ESEM using 30 kV accelerating voltage. Sapphire EDAX attachment of the SEM was used to perform the EDS analysis. Conventional transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images of the samples spread on carbon coated Cu grids were recorded using Carl Zeiss instrument (120 kV). High resolution TEM was done on Jeol-2100F instrument. NETZSCH STA 409 PC instrument was used for the thermogravimetric (TG) studies. The temperature range was between 30800 C and the heating was done under air at a rate of 3 C min<small><sup>1</sup></small>. The specific surface area has been calculated using the BET (BrunauerEmmettTeller) method and the N<small><sub>2</sub></small> adsorption data was recorded in a Bel Japan Inc., Belsorp II surface area analyzer. HOBIBA LabRAM HR 800 spectrometer with <span class="italic"></span><small><sub>exc</sub></small> = 632.8 nm laser source was used to record the Raman spectra of the samples. XPS (X-ray photoelectron spectra) was obtained using Al K<small><sub></sub></small> excitation source in AXIS Supra, Kratos Analytical, SHIMADZU instrument.</span>

<h3 id="sect389"><span class="b_heading">Electrochemical measurements</span></h3>
<span>The working anodes for LIB and SIB were prepared in the following manner. The active material, carbon black (conducting agent) and sodium alginate (binder) was mixed in de-ionized (DI) water in 8<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1 ratio according to their weight%. The paste obtained was smeared on a Cu foil current collector using doctor blade. The area of each electrode was 1 cm<small><sup>2</sup></small> and they were dried for 8 h in a hot air oven maintained at 50 C. The electrolyte used was 1 M LiPF<small><sub>6</sub></small> in a 50<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">50 (v/v) mixture of dimethyl carbonate (DMC) and ethylene carbonate (EC) for Li-ion coin cell and 1 M NaClO<small><sub>4</sub></small> in a 50<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">50 (v/v) mixture of propylene carbonate (PC) and EC having 5 wt% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) added to it in case of Na-ion coin cell. The CR2032 coin cells were assembled in an Argon flushed glove box (Mbraun, Unilab, Germany) using the corresponding anode, Whatman glass microfiber filters GF/D separator soaked in electrolyte and Li/Na foil as counter/reference electrode. Full cell was constructed using Na<small><sub>3</sub></small>V<small><sub>2</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>3</sub></small> (NVP) cathode and the 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite as anode using procedure as reported earlier.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit33"><sup><span class="sup_ref">33</span></sup></a> Pre-sodiated anode (5 cycles at 0.2 Ag<small><sup>1</sup></small>) was used to compensate for the initial coulombic efficiency loss. The mass ratio of cathode: anode was fixed at 4<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1. The electrolyte was same as that used for assembling the Na ion half-cell. Neware Battery Test System from Shenzhen Neware Electronic Co, China has been used to record the galvanostatic charge/discharge measurements at room temperature within a voltage window of 0.13 V and at various current densities. The specific capacity reported for all the anodes is referenced with respect to the active material mass in the electrode. The cyclic voltammetry (CV) studies were done at 0.1 mV s<small><sup>1</sup></small> (voltage range of 0.13 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Li/Li<small><sup>+</sup></small> and 0.12.7 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na/Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>) on a Biologic potentiostat/galvanostat (BCS 810) instrument. Electrochemical impedance spectra (EIS) were recorded on a Novocontrol Alpha-A High Frequency Analyzer (frequency range = 1 Hz to 1 MHz).</span>


<h2 id="sect408"><span class="a_heading">Results and discussion</span></h2>
<span>CuS was synthesized <span class="italic">via</span> precipitation method using copper(<span class="small_caps">II</span>) nitrate and sodium thiosulphate as precursors which was stirred under N<small><sub>2</sub></small> cover at 90 C for 7 h. The reactions leading to the synthesis is given below (<a title="" href="#eqn1">eqn (1)</a> and <a title="" href="#eqn2">2</a>):<table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn1">
<span id="eqn1">Cu(NO<small><sub>3</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small>3H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O + 2Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>S<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>3</sub></small> = Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>[Cu(S<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>3</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small>] + 2NaNO<small><sub>3</sub></small> + 3H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O</span>
</span></td><td class="rightEqn">(1)</td></tr></tbody></table><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn2">
<span id="eqn2">Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>[Cu(S<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>3</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small>] + H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O  CuS + H<small><sub>2</sub></small>SO<small><sub>4</sub></small> + Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>SO<small><sub>4</sub></small></span>
</span></td><td class="rightEqn">(2)</td></tr></tbody></table></span>
<p class="otherpara">The synthesis procedure of the CuS/g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite samples is schematically represented in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1</a>. The powder XRD of CuS, g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> and CuS/g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite is shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2a</a>. The diffraction pattern corresponds to the hexagonal phase of covellite CuS (PCPDF# 782121). g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> exhibits a sharp strong peak at 27.3 and a weak broad one at 13.2 pertaining to the (002) and (110) diffraction planes, respectively. The strong peak is due to in-plane periodic arrangement of tris-triazine units of g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> and the weak peak arises due to the inter-planar arrangement of the aromatic rings.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit30"><sup><span class="sup_ref">30</span></sup></a> The CuS/g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite displays peaks pertaining to both CuS and g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> and a narrower peak at 13.2 is observed for the composite compared to pristine g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small>. This indicates that the interplanar arrangement of the aromatic rings in g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> gets more pronounced in the composite which can be ascribed to the anchoring of the CuS particles on them. The morphology of CuS and CuS/g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite is visible from the SEM images shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2b and c</a>, respectively. CuS shows spherical ball like formation whereas in case of the composite, the CuS particles gets engulfed in the g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> nanosheets. The presence of Cu, S, C and N along with a minor fraction of O element is seen in the EDS spectrum of the composite (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2d</a>). The presence of oxygen is probably due to exposure of the sample to air and moisture. Cu and S are present in the composite in 1<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1.15 atomic ratio. In contrast, the EDS of CuS confirms presence of only Cu and S elements in a 1<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1.1 atomic ratio (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2e</a>). The TEM images of CuS gives a clearer picture of its actual morphology and it is seen that the particles are spherical in shape with diameter ranging between 1025 nm (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2f</a>). In case of the CuS/g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite, the TEM image confirms the presence of CuS nanoparticles within the g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> sheets (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2g</a>). The diameter of the CuS nanoparticles in the composite range between 1015 nm and the smaller size compared to pristine CuS is attributed to the presence of g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> nanosheets which arrests further growth of the nanoparticles. The HRTEM image of the CuS/g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite is shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2h</a>. The lattice spacing of 0.28 nm corresponding to the (103) plane of CuS nanoparticle is clearly discernible in the g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> matrix. Further confirmation for the inclusion of CuS nanoparticles in g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> matrix in the composite is obtained from Raman spectroscopy. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2i</a> shows the Raman plots of CuS, g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> and the 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite. A peak at 468.6 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small> is seen in the Raman plot of CuS which is the optical phonon mode corresponding to SS stretching.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit34"><sup><span class="sup_ref">34</span></sup></a> The sharpness of the peak indicates the crystalline nature of the sample. The CuS vibrational stretching occur at 118 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small> and 266.2 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit35"><sup><span class="sup_ref">35</span></sup></a> These low frequency vibration modes are generally observed in case of nano sized CuS.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit36"><sup><span class="sup_ref">36</span></sup></a> For pristine g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small>, the bending vibration corresponding to <img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">C(sp<small><sup>2</sup></small>) is observed at 1241 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small>. The blue shifting of this peak with respect to that seen in the bulk g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> (1234 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small>), indicates phonon confinement pertaining to the formation of nanosheet-like structure.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit37"><sup><span class="sup_ref">37</span></sup></a> Other peaks corresponding to various CN vibrations are observed at 479.1, 542.9, 702.2 and 748.8 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small>. The signature peaks of both CuS and g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> are observed in the composite sample. The intensity of the CuS peaks are stronger which can be ascribed to its higher relative concentration in the CuS/g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite. To quantify the proportion of g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> in the composite, thermogravimetric studies was done under air and the corresponding TG plot is shown in Fig. S1a.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> CuS shows a slight mass loss at 275 C followed by an increase in mass which is ascribed to loss of sulfur from CuS leading to formation of Cu<small><sub>2</sub></small>S and then conversion of CuS/Cu<small><sub>2</sub></small>S to CuSO<small><sub>4</sub></small>, respectively.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit38"><sup><span class="sup_ref">38</span></sup></a> The final mass loss between 610827 C is due to decomposition of CuSO<small><sub>4</sub></small> to CuO and the total weight loss is found to be 16.6%. The TG curve of g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> show 100% weight loss between 580750 C and is due to its decomposition. For the CuS/g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite, the total weight loss observed at 800 C is 33.2% which indicates that the sample has approximately 20% of g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> by weight and this is in accordance with the nominal composition.</p>
<br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig1"><a href="/image/article/2022/RA/d2ra02014a/d2ra02014a-f1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d2ra02014a-f1.tif" src="/image/article/2022/RA/d2ra02014a/d2ra02014a-f1.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 1 </b> <span id="fig1"><span class="graphic_title">Schematic representation of the synthesis of CuS/g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
<br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig2"><a href="/image/article/2022/RA/d2ra02014a/d2ra02014a-f2_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d2ra02014a-f2.tif" src="/image/article/2022/RA/d2ra02014a/d2ra02014a-f2.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 2 </b> <span id="fig2"><span class="graphic_title">(a) Powder XRD of g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small>, CuS, 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small>; (b) SEM of CuS; (c) SEM of 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small>; (d) EDS of CuS; (e) EDS of 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small>; (f) TEM of CuS; (g) TEM of 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small>; (h) HRTEM of 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small>; (i) Raman spectra of g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small>, CuS, 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small>.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
<p class="otherpara">X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) has been used to study the chemical and electronic state of the elements present in the CuS/g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite. The survey spectra of CuS, g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> and 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> is shown in Fig. S1b<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> and it shows presence of oxygen in all the samples apart from the expected Cu and S in CuS, N and C in g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> and C, N, Cu, S in the 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite. The O 1s peak at 532.1 eV is due to the adsorption of atmospheric oxygen on the sample surface.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit30"><sup><span class="sup_ref">30</span></sup></a> The C 1s peak seen in the pristine CuS sample at 284.5 eV is attributed to the internal standard used for reference. The survey spectra indicate the absence of any other elemental impurities in the samples. The high resolution XPS of Cu2p in CuS and 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> shows the presence of Cu2p<small><sub>3/2</sub></small> and Cu2p<small><sub>1/2</sub></small> peaks (Fig. S1c<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). For CuS, the Cu2p<small><sub>3/2</sub></small> and Cu2p<small><sub>1/2</sub></small> peaks are observed at 932.4 eV and 952.5 eV, respectively, which indicates presence of Cu in predominantly +1 oxidation state.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit39"><sup><span class="sup_ref">39</span></sup></a> In the 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite, broader peaks pertaining to Cu2p<small><sub>3/2</sub></small> and Cu2p<small><sub>1/2</sub></small> are observed at 932.1 eV and 951.9 eV, respectively along with a weak satellite peak at 945.4 eV which indicates the presence of Cu in both +1 and +2 oxidation states.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit40"><sup><span class="sup_ref">40</span></sup></a> The presence of Cu<small><sup>2+</sup></small> can be attributed to surface oxidation. The shift in the Cu 2p<small><sub>3/2</sub></small> and Cu/S 2p<small><sub>1/2</sub></small> peaks to lower binding energy in the composite sample and its broadness compared to pristine CuS has been reported in literature and indicates slight chemical interaction between CuS and g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit30"><sup><span class="sup_ref">30,31</span></sup></a> Fig. S1d<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> depicts the high resolution S2p XPS of CuS. The spectrum obtained could be fitted to S<small><sup>2</sup></small> 2p<small><sub>3/2</sub></small> and 2p<small><sub>1/2</sub></small> peaks at 160.9 and 161.5 eV and (S<small><sub>2</sub></small>)<small><sup>2</sup></small> 2p<small><sub>3/2</sub></small> and 2p<small><sub>1/2</sub></small> peaks at 161.8 and 162.9 eV, respectively. The ratio of area of the sulfide to disulfide peaks is 1<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1.9 which is close to that expected for covellite CuS. Another broad peak at a binding energy of 163.5 eV is also seen which has been assigned to elemental sulfur. The results obtained are similar to that reported in earlier literature.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit39"><sup><span class="sup_ref">39</span></sup></a> In case of the 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small>composite, S<small><sup>2</sup></small> 2p<small><sub>3/2</sub></small> and 2p<small><sub>1/2</sub></small> peaks are seen at 160.7 and 161.4 eV and (S<small><sub>2</sub></small>)<small><sup>2</sup></small> 2p<small><sub>3/2</sub></small> and 2p<small><sub>1/2</sub></small> peaks at 161.6 and 162.7 eV, respectively, and this shift to lower binding energy has been previously reported for CuS/g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composites (Fig. S1e<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit30"><sup><span class="sup_ref">30</span></sup></a> The high resolution N 1s peaks for g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> and 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> could be fitted to two peaks at 398.5 eV and 400.4 eV pertaining to sp<small><sup>2</sup></small> N atoms of (CN<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">C) moiety and sp<small><sup>3</sup></small> N of the N-(C)<small><sub>3</sub></small> group, respectively (Fig. S1f<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). The higher intensity of the former peak indicates presence of higher proportion of pyridinic N in g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> which is beneficial for Li<small><sup>+</sup></small> ion transport and also leads to better electronic conductivity in both the samples.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit27"><sup><span class="sup_ref">27</span></sup></a> The relative concentration of N-(C)<small><sub>3</sub></small> group is further reduced in the composite sample and hence it is expected to be a better anode material compared to pristine g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small>. The high resolution C 1s spectra of g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> and 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> shows major peak at 288.3 eV and 288.7 eV, respectively and they are ascribed to NC<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">N carbon (Fig. S1g<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit41"><sup><span class="sup_ref">41</span></sup></a> Hence, both C 1s and N 1s high resolution XPS of 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> indicates presence of higher proportion of pyridinic N bonds which is desirable for its Li<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> ion storage application.</p>
<p class="otherpara">The specific surface area of CuS, g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> and 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> has been calculated <span class="italic">via</span> BET method from the Type IV N<small><sub>2</sub></small> adsorption/desorption isotherms with H4 pattern of hysteresis loop obtained in case of all the samples (Fig. S2a<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). CuS has a specific surface area of 30.21 m<small><sup>2</sup></small> g<small><sup>1</sup></small> which is higher than that reported for CuS obtained <span class="italic">via</span> precipitation technique (9.56 m<small><sup>2</sup></small> g<small><sup>1</sup></small>) and <span class="italic">via</span> hydrothermal route (14.5 m<small><sup>2</sup></small> g<small><sup>1</sup></small>) but less than that obtained using acid mediated dealloying reaction of Ti<small><sub>60</sub></small>Cu<small><sub>40</sub></small> (48.267.9 m<small><sup>2</sup></small> g<small><sup>1</sup></small>).<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit17"><sup><span class="sup_ref">17,30,31,42</span></sup></a> The slow and controlled addition of reactants with high speed stirring leads to smaller size of CuS nanoparticles and increases the surface area in our sample.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit43"><sup><span class="sup_ref">43</span></sup></a> The specific surface area of the 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite sample is 46.63 m<small><sup>2</sup></small> g<small><sup>1</sup></small> which is due to the dispersion of CuS nanoparticles on the g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> nanosheets. This results in an increase in the surface area of the composite compared to pristine CuS but a decrease compared to that calculated for g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> (72.15 m<small><sup>2</sup></small> g<small><sup>1</sup></small>) since CuS blocks the pores present in g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small>. The larger surface area of the composite improves its ability to provide adequate migration channels for Li<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> ions and leads to its better wettability by the electrolyte and can reduce electrode resistance.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit23"><sup><span class="sup_ref">23</span></sup></a> The total pore volume of the samples has been obtained <span class="italic">via</span> BarrettJoynerHalenda (BJH) method from the corresponding pore size distribution plots (Fig. S2b<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). The pore size distribution varies mostly between 570 nm for all the samples confirming the presence of mesopores and the total pore volume is highest for g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> (1.405 cm<small><sup>3</sup></small> g<small><sup>1</sup></small>). Pristine CuS has a pore volume of 0.695 cm<small><sup>3</sup></small> g<small><sup>1</sup></small> which can be attributed to the interparticle space. The composite has a higher total pore volume of 0.923 cm<small><sup>3</sup></small> g<small><sup>1</sup></small> compared to CuS but it is less than that of g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> and this corroborates with the relative specific surface area obtained for all the three samples. The increase in total pore volume and specific surface area in the composite is beneficial for accommodating the volume expansion observed in CuS anodes during cycling.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit23"><sup><span class="sup_ref">23</span></sup></a> This can lead to better cycling performance of the composite by preserving the electrode structure.</p>
<p class="otherpara">The CV plots of pristine CuS and 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite at scan rate of 0.1 mV s<small><sup>1</sup></small> while functioning as anode in LIB configuration is shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3a and b</a>, respectively. Reduction peaks are seen at 2.11 eV and 1.62 eV during 1<small><sup>st</sup></small> cycle discharge which is due to formation of Cu<small><sub>2</sub></small>S and its subsequent conversion to yield Li<small><sub>2</sub></small>S and Cu, respectively.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit44"><sup><span class="sup_ref">44</span></sup></a> Apart from this, a broad peak between 0.30.9 eV with weak intensity is seen which disappears in the consecutive cycles and is assigned to the formation of SEI layer.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit18"><sup><span class="sup_ref">18</span></sup></a> The oxidation peaks are seen at 1.91 eV and 2.37 eV which is attributed to formation of Cu<small><sub>2<span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>S and the overpotential pertaining to Li<small><sub>2</sub></small>S, respectively.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit44"><sup><span class="sup_ref">44</span></sup></a> From second cycle onwards, the cathodic peak at 2.11 eV disappears and this is attributed to the irreversible nature of the reaction leading to the formation of Cu<small><sub>2</sub></small>S:</p><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn3">
<span id="eqn3">2CuS + 2Li  Cu<small><sub>2</sub></small>S + Li<small><sub>2</sub></small>S</span>
</span></td><td class="rightEqn">(3)</td></tr></tbody></table><p></p>
<br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig3"><a href="/image/article/2022/RA/d2ra02014a/d2ra02014a-f3_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d2ra02014a-f3.tif" src="/image/article/2022/RA/d2ra02014a/d2ra02014a-f3.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 3 </b> <span id="fig3"><span class="graphic_title">In LIB half cell: (a) CV plot of CuS; (b) CV plot of 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small>; (c) galvanostatic discharge charge of CuS; (d) galvanostatic discharge charge of 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small>; (e) discharge capacity and coulombic efficiency plot, (f) rate capability, (g) long term cyclability of CuS and 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small>; (h) SEM of as prepared and after 1000 cycle run of CuS and 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> electrode.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
<p class="otherpara">The anodic peak at 2.37 eV pertaining to Li<small><sub>2</sub></small>S overpotential exhibits a sharp decrease in intensity with cycling and this is similar to that observed in earlier reports.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit44"><sup><span class="sup_ref">44</span></sup></a> For the 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite, the area covered under the CV plot is larger than that observed in case of pristine CuS and this indicates an increase in the capacity of the composite anode (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3b</a>).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit28"><sup><span class="sup_ref">28</span></sup></a> The broad and very weak cathodic peak at 2.31 eV pertaining to Cu<small><sub>2</sub></small>S formation disappears from the second cycle and the strong cathodic peak at 1.56 eV denoting further reduction to form Cu and Li<small><sub>2</sub></small>S persists but shift to slightly higher value of 1.57 eV. The anodic peaks are seen at 1.96 eV and 2.37 eV and is ascribed to the reactions leading to formation of Cu<small><sub>2</sub></small>S. There is almost no change in CV plot of the 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite from second cycle onwards which indicate its better electrochemical stability compared to the pristine CuS electrode.</p>
<p class="otherpara">The galvanostatic discharge/charge (GDC) plots corresponding to few representative cycles of the CuS and 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite anode in LIB mode within the voltage range of 0.013.0 V is shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3c and d</a>, respectively. The GDC experiments were performed under current density of 0.1 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>. CuS exhibits a first discharge/charge capacity of 930/693 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> which decreases to 369/353 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> by the 20<small><sup>th</sup></small> cycle. The discharge plot exhibits plateaus at 2.1 and 1.6 eV during the first cycle but the former plateau region is not seen in the consecutive cycles. In case of the charge plots, the plateau region is seen at 1.9 and 2.4 eV in the first cycle with considerable reduction in the stretch of plateau region in the consecutive cycles. The results obtained match with the cathodic and anodic peak positions observed in the CV plot of pristine CuS. The plateau region in GDC plots becomes almost indiscernible by the 20<small><sup>th</sup></small> cycle and considerable loss in specific capacity is observed by the 200<small><sup>th</sup></small> run. The first discharge plot of the 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite exhibits two voltage plateaus at 2.3 and 1.5 eV whereas the discharge plots for the second and third cycle shows only one voltage plateau at 1.5 eV. The charge plots of the composite exhibit voltage plateaus at 1.9 and 2.37 eV but the length of the plateau region diminishes with cycling. The results agree with that observed in the CV plots of the composite sample. The first cycle discharge/charge renders a specific capacity of 981/855 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> yielding an initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 87.15%. The ICE is higher in case of the composite sample compared to that observed in pristine CuS (74.5%). The irreversible loss in the first cycle is caused by formation of the SEI layer with concomitant electrolyte breakdown and the irreversible nature of reaction between Li and CuS due to formation of polysulfides.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit19"><sup><span class="sup_ref">19</span></sup></a> Higher ICE value in the composite indicates better stability of SEI which can be attributed to the presence of g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> which restricts the polysulfide intermediates from diffusing out into the electrolyte. The presence of the pyridinic N atoms in g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> binds to the lithium ions of the polar polysulfide species and restricts its diffusion.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit45"><sup><span class="sup_ref">45</span></sup></a> The coulombic efficiency (CE) of both CuS and the 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite increases to 94.8% and 96.2%, respectively, by the 20<small><sup>th</sup></small> run (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3e</a>). However, compared to the first cycle charge capacity, the capacity retention after a run of 200 cycles is 81.4% for the composite which is much higher than obtained in case of pristine CuS anode (44.5%) and is a signature of its better electrochemical property (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3e</a>). The anode with g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> nanosheets as active material exhibits a specific capacity of 202 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> (current density = 0.1 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, 200 cycles) in LIB configuration with a CE of 99.56% by the 3<small><sup>rd</sup></small> cycle which matches with earlier reports and establishes its suitability as a composite host for CuS nanoparticles (Fig. S3<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit12"><sup><span class="sup_ref">12</span></sup></a> The specific charge capacity of the 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> anode after 200 cycles is 699.3 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> which is enhanced drastically compared to 308.6 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> delivered by the pristine CuS anode (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3e</a>). The reversible capacity of the pristine CuS anode is less than that observed for anodes with CuS nano-rods/wires but more than that with CuS microflowers, which highlights the role of nanostructures in the final reversible capacity delivered (Table S1<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). The higher surface area and enhanced pore volume of the 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite shortens the diffusion path of the Li<small><sup>+</sup></small> ions and ensures its better wettability by the electrolyte which accounts for its higher specific capacity compared to pristine CuS.</p>
<p class="otherpara">The rate capability of pristine CuS and the 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> anode has been tested at 0.1, 0.3, 0.5, 1 and 2 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small> current density <span class="italic">via</span> GDC within 0.013 V range (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3f</a>). The first 20 cycles of discharge/charge were at 0.1 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small> and for all other current density, the anodes were cycled for 10 runs. At 2 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small> current density, the 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> anode displayed a reversible capacity of 477.5 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> and maintained a CE of 99.6%. On immediate reduction of current density to 0.1 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, the reversible capacity increased to 657.5 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> by the 70<small><sup>th</sup></small> cycle run for the 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> anode which is slightly higher than that observed in case of its 20<small><sup>th</sup></small> cycle run at similar current density. This indicates activation of the electrode and also its stability inspite of repeated volume alteration during the lithiation/de-lithiation process. For the pristine CuS anode, increasing the current rate to 2 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small> yielded a meagre reversible capacity of 142.9 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> with CE of 96.2%. The value of both the parameters are reduced compared to that of the composite anode. On decreasing current to 0.1 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, the reversible capacity increases to 352.9 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> which is slightly lower than that obtained in the 20<small><sup>th</sup></small> cycle run of the anode (357.9 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>). The 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite anode shows 25% loss in capacity on increase in current rate from 0.1 to 2 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small> as opposed to 60% capacity loss exhibited by pristine CuS under similar variation in current rate. Approximately 60% loss in specific capacity with similar current rate variation has been reported in case of small CuS nanorods.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit46"><sup><span class="sup_ref">46</span></sup></a> Hence, it can be deduced that addition of g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> support to CuS, improves its rate capability.</p>
<p class="otherpara">The cycling ability of both pristine CuS and 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite anode at a moderately high current rate of 1 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small> has been tested for 1000 cycles and the matching plot is shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3g</a>. The first 25 cycle run is at a current of 0.1 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small> followed by 10 cycles at 0.5 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small> and this was done to activate the electrodes. The 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite anode shows appreciable stability and delivers a reversible capacity of 551 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at the end of the 1000<small><sup>th</sup></small> cycle. Compared to it, the capacity delivered by the pristine CuS anode keeps fluctuating and decreases to 104 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> after 1000 cycles. The post mortem analysis of the electrodes after 1000 runs has been done <span class="italic">via</span> SEM and has been compared with the as prepared electrodes (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3h</a>). In case of the CuS electrode, cracks and fissures are seen after 1000 cycle run which peels off the active material from the Cu contact and cause fluctuation as well as reduction in its capacity. In case of the 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite electrode, extensive pulverization is seen after 1000 cycles but no obvious discontinuity or gaps is visible in the electrode. Hence, addition of g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> helps in withstanding the structural stress brought about by the volume alteration during the lithiation/de-lithiation process in CuS.</p>
<p class="otherpara">The evaluation of reaction kinetics in the CuS and 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite anode has been done <span class="italic">via</span> electrochemical impedance studies. The electrochemical impedance spectra (EIS) for both anodes (as-prepared and after a run of 100 cycles) have been recorded within a frequency spread of 0.11 MHz and the Nyquist plots is shown in Fig. S4.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> The inset of Fig. S4<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> depicts the corresponding equivalent circuit where <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>s</sub></small>, <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>cf</sub></small> and <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>ct</sub></small> denotes the resistance arising due to the electrode and electrolyte, the impedance due to SEI formation and the charge transfer resistance arising due to Li<small><sup>+</sup></small> diffusion between electrolyte and electrode, respectively. The straight line obtained in the low frequency part of the Nyquist plot is due to Warburg impedance (<span class="italic">W</span>) pertaining to Li<small><sup>+</sup></small> ion diffusion. The equivalent circuit includes a constant phase element (CPE) pertaining to the formation of SEI layer, and also double layer capacitance (<span class="italic">C</span>). The fitting of the equivalent circuit to the Nyquist plot has been done using EIS Spectrum Analyser software and the corresponding values of <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>s</sub></small>, <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>cf</sub></small> and <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>ct</sub></small> are given in <a title="Select to navigate to table" href="#tab1">Table 1</a>. Both anodes show lower <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>cf</sub></small> and <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>ct</sub></small> values after 100 runs compared to the fresh as-prepared electrodes suggesting lower interface and charge transfer impedance which is due to subtle changes in the SEI layer and better kinetics of the conversion reaction with repeated cycling, respectively. Similar results have been documented in case of heterostructured FeS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/CuS anodes.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit47"><sup><span class="sup_ref">47</span></sup></a> The pristine CuS anode has higher <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>ct</sub></small> and <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>s</sub></small> compared to the composite both before and after cycling which suggests that the presence of g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> reduces the charge transfer impedance by providing better electrode/electrolyte interaction, enhances the electronic conductivity and promotes Li<small><sup>+</sup></small> diffusion in the sample. <a title="" href="#eqn4">Eqn (4)</a> has been used to estimate the Li-ion diffusion coefficient (<span class="italic">D</span><small><sub>Li<small><sup>+</sup></small></sub></small>) in CuS and 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite anode both before and after a run of 100 cycles:</p><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn4">
<a href="/image/article/2022/RA/d2ra02014a/d2ra02014a-t1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d2ra02014a-t1.tif" src="/image/article/2022/RA/d2ra02014a/d2ra02014a-t1.gif"></a></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(4)</td></tr></tbody></table><p></p>

<p class="otherpara">In the above equation, <span class="italic">T</span>, <span class="italic">R</span>, <span class="italic">A</span>, <span class="italic">C</span> and <span class="italic">F</span> represents absolute temperature, gas constant, area of anode covered with active material (1 cm<small><sup>2</sup></small>), Li-ion molar density in electrode and Faraday constant, respectively. <span class="italic">n</span> denotes number of electrons involved in the half reaction during redox process, and <span class="italic"></span> is Warburg factor which is the slope of the <span class="italic">Z</span> <span class="italic">vs. </span><small><sup>1/2</sup></small> plot as given in <a title="" href="#eqn5">eqn (5)</a></p><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn5">
<span id="eqn5"><span class="italic">Z</span> = <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>D</sub></small> + <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>L</sub></small> + <span class="italic"></span><small><sup>1/2</sup></small></span>
</span></td><td class="rightEqn">(5)</td></tr></tbody></table><p></p>
<p class="otherpara">The <span class="italic"></span> and <span class="italic">D</span><small><sub>Li<small><sup>+</sup></small></sub></small> values are listed in <a title="Select to navigate to table" href="#tab1">Table 1</a> and the <span class="italic">D</span><small><sub>Li<small><sup>+</sup></small></sub></small> values are higher in case of the 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite anode both before and after cycling compared to the CuS anode. This confirms the faster Li<small><sup>+</sup></small> ion diffusion in the composite which supports the results obtained from the Nyquist plots.</p>
<p class="otherpara">The electrochemical performance of 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite anode has been compared with other CuS anodes reported in literature (Table S2<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). It can be deduced that the 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite has much enhanced electrochemical properties compared to that reported for most of the graphene and reduced graphene oxide based composites of CuS. The initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 87.2% obtained in the 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite anode is the highest among all the CuS composites reported and that exhibited by the pure CuS anode (74.5%). This indicates that the dispersion of CuS nanoparticles on g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> nanosheets leads to an improvement in its electrochemical performance.</p>
<p class="otherpara">Conversion type anode materials like CuS have better applicability in SIBs compared to intercalation type materials like graphite due to the larger size of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> ions compared to Li<small><sup>+</sup></small> ions which negatively impacts its movement. Hence it was of interest to study the electrochemical performance of both CuS and the 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite as anode materials for SIB application. The CV of CuS exhibits multiple cathodic and anodic peaks which are not reversible for the first three cycles (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4a</a>) suggesting multistep process for the electrochemical reaction.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit13"><sup><span class="sup_ref">13</span></sup></a> During the first scan, cathodic peaks are seen at 0.55 V, 1.44 V and 1.87 V. During the subsequent scans, the peak at 0.55 V is not seen which indicates that it is due to the irreversible SEI layer formation. The other two peaks are due to the insertion of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> ions into CuS to form Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>S and Cu. The first anodic scan exhibits a broad peak at 1.72 V and two more peaks at 2.13 V and 2.21 V which are different from that observed in subsequent scans. The CV plots clearly indicate irreversibility and some structure change in the material. The anodic peaks seen at 1.65 V and 2.19 V during the third scan pertains to formation of Cu<small><sub>2</sub></small>S from Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>S and Cu.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit48"><sup><span class="sup_ref">48</span></sup></a> The corresponding discharge/charge curves for CuS for the first three cycles is shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4c</a>. The discharge and charge plateaus match with the peaks observed in CV. The ICE obtained for this CuS anode is 86% which is comparable to that stated in earlier reports (Table S3<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). The coincidence of CV plots for the 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite is better than that observed for the pristine CuS anode suggesting decreased irreversible loss and better structure stability (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4b</a>). The broad cathodic peak observed between 0.250.35 V observed in the composite in the first cycle is due to SEI layer formation. The slight loss in intensity of the anodic peaks during second and third cycle indicate specific capacity fading. The cathodic peaks at 1.24 V and 1.89 V are attributed to Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> insertion into the CuS/g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite structure with the concomitant formation of Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>S whereas the broad anodic peaks at 1.60 V and 2.20 V is indicative of Cu<small><sub>2</sub></small>S formation.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit48"><sup><span class="sup_ref">48</span></sup></a> The galvanostatic discharge/charge plots exhibit multiple plateau region which corroborates with the results obtained from CV studies (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4d</a>). Overlapping of the plateau regions in the second and third cycle indicate enhanced reversibility in the 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite compared to that seen in case of pristine CuS. The cycling performance of CuS and 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> at 0.1 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small> is shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4e</a>. After 200 cycles, the specific capacity retention is 40.6% and 63.4% compared to the first cycle for CuS and 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small>, respectively. A rapid decrease in charge capacity is observed in the first 25 cycles for CuS electrode which then increases unevenly up to 170 cycles and drops again and is a signature of electrochemical activation resulting from constant disintegration and reformation of active material to generate fresh sites for Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> ion storage.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit12"><sup><span class="sup_ref">12</span></sup></a> The deleterious effect of volume expansion leading to rapidly changing specific capacity during cycling is buffered by the presence of g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> support in the 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite. Consequently, the cycling performance is quite steady in case of the composite and the enhanced specific capacity (479.5 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> after 200 cycles at 0.1 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>) is attributed to higher surface area and pore volume which ensures higher number of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> ions storage sites and better electrode/electrolyte contact. The 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite also exhibits better rate performance compared to the CuS electrode (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4f</a>). In case of CuS, the specific reversible capacity (mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>)/current rate (A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>) obtained varies as 410/0.1, 312/0.3, 276/0.5, 246/1, 190/2 and reverts to 316 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> on reducing the current rate to 0.1 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>. For the composite, the specific reversible capacity decreases from 508 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at current rate of 0.1 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small> to 408 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at current rate of 2 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>. On decreasing current rate back to 0.1 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, the reversible capacity obtained is 531 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> which is slightly higher than the initially obtained value and can be attributed to the better electrochemical activation of the cycled electrode. Long term cycling of both electrodes has been studied at a higher current rate of 1 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small> after initial activation of the electrode at 0.1 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small> for 10 cycles and 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite exhibits stable performance with a reversible capacity of 424 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> after a total run of 800 cycles (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4g</a>). The CuS electrode runs for 800 cycles but the specific capacity is lower than the composite and exhibit considerable fluctuation. The post mortem SEM of both electrodes after 800 cycles is shown in Fig. S5a and b.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> Deep fissures and cracks are evident in the cycled CuS electrode which is not conducive for its electrochemical performance. In case of the 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite, the surface of the electrode turns uneven but the absence of any major gaps ensures better connectivity and stability in the structure which augments the capacity and rate performance. The Nyquist plots for as prepared CuS and 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite and after 100 cycles is shown is <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4h</a>. The semicircle observed at high and intermediate frequencies correspond to the resistance due to SEI layer formation and charge transfer and the straight line at low frequency is attributed to the diffusion of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> ions in the half cell. The corresponding equivalent circuit shown in Fig. S6<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> has been obtained by fitting the Nyquist plots by means of EIS Spectrum Analyser software and the values of <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>s</sub></small>, <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>cf</sub></small> and <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>ct</sub></small> is listed in <a title="Select to navigate to table" href="#tab2">Table 2</a>. The <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>ct</sub></small> value of the composite is lower than that of pristine CuS both in as prepared and after 100 cycles which indicates an improvement in the conductivity of the former. Also, the steeper gradient of the oblique line of the composite compared to pristine CuS at low frequency before the testing indicate better diffusion of sodium ions in the former which is due to the even spreading of the CuS particles in the g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> matrix leading to an open structure. The remarkable decrease in <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>ct</sub></small> values on cycling of the CuS as well as 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> composite electrode has been reported earlier and has been attributed to the formation of Cu<small><sub>2</sub></small>S which has higher electrical conductivity.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit49"><sup><span class="sup_ref">49</span></sup></a> The calculated D<small><sub>Na</sub></small><small><sup>+</sup></small> values are also given in <a title="Select to navigate to table" href="#tab2">Table 2</a> and the improvement in Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> ion diffusion coefficient in the composite is indicative of the support extended by g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> in the ion transfer process.</p>
<br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig4"><a href="/image/article/2022/RA/d2ra02014a/d2ra02014a-f4_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d2ra02014a-f4.tif" src="/image/article/2022/RA/d2ra02014a/d2ra02014a-f4.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 4 </b> <span id="fig4"><span class="graphic_title">In SIB half cell: (a) CV plot of CuS; (b) CV plot of 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small>; (c) galvanostatic discharge charge of CuS; (d) galvanostatic discharge charge of 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small>; (e) discharge capacity and coulombic efficiency plot, (f) rate capability, (g) long term cyclability of CuS and 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small>; (h) Nyquist plots of as prepared and after 100 cycle run of CuS and 0.8CuS:0.2g-C<small><sub>3</sub></small>N<small><sub>4</sub></small> electrode.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
